#### 3.2 Route learning

Since detailed sensory information cannot be recalled at very high capacities, route learning is performed by learning associations between the *recollection* of the sensory inputs at a location ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-30.gif)

, and the velocity shift vector ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-31.gif)

determining the direction of motion of the trajectory being learned at that location. This association can be learned directly through pseudoinverse learning as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-32.gif)

where, *S*<sub><em>r</em></sub> is a *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> ×*N*<sub><em>seq</em></sub> dimensional matrix with columns as the recalled sensory inputs ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-32.gif)

, and *V* is a 6 ×*N*<sub><em>seq</em></sub> dimensional matrix with columns as the corresponding velocities. These associations can then be used to recall long trajectories through ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-33.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-34.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-35.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-36.gif)

As argued in Sec. D.6, this results in perfect sequence recall for *N*<sub><em>seq</em></sub> ≤ *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub>, which can scale as the exponentially large capacity of the grid coding space.

#### 3.3 Goal and context-based remapping

When initialized in a new environment, we model the grid state population activity to be randomly initialized in the grid-coding space (a mechanistic model for such random initialization will be discussed in future work), i.e., the grid state undergoes *remapping*. This grid coding state, along with the corresponding hippocampal coding state and sensory observations at that location are then stored in the corresponding weight matrices, i.e., *W*<sub><em>hs</em></sub> and *W*<sub><em>sh</em></sub>, via [Eqs. (7](#disp-formula-7)\-[8](#disp-formula-8)). When brought back to a previously seen environment, these weight matrices in Vector-HaSH use the observed sensory observations to drive the hippocampal cell (and hence grid cell) population activity to the state initialized at the first traversal of that environment.

Similar to new environments, we also model contextual information (such as goals, rewards, start-end location pairs) to be appended to the sensory inputs. We allow new contextual information to also trigger reinitialization of grid state, which then permits storage of multiple paths that involve the same spatial location, provided that they are distinguished by a contextual signal.

We use this set up of manual reinitialization of the grid state to reproduce the experimental observations of splitter cells<sup><a id="xref-ref-110-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-110">110</a></sup>, route dependent place cells<sup><a id="xref-ref-111-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-111">111</a></sup>, directional place fields in one-dimensional environments<sup><a id="xref-ref-112-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-112">112</a></sup> and on directed routes in two-dimensional environments<sup><a id="xref-ref-113-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-113">113</a></sup> in [Fig. 7c-j](#F7); and of directional place fields in a radial eight-arm maze<sup><a id="xref-ref-113-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-113">113</a></sup> in [Fig. S17](#F25). In all of these cases, we first generate trajectories corresponding to the paths that the animals are constrained to traverse in the given experiment. These trajectories, are then stored in Vector-HaSH at a random location in the grid coding space through a path learning mechanism. At new contexts, the grid state in the model is reinitialized and the agent then continues at a new location in the grid coding space. This results in different spatial firing fields, irrespective of whether the agent is at the same spatial location as in a different previous context.

For all the simulations in [Fig. 7c-j](#F7) and [Fig. S17](#F25), Vector-HaSH with *λ* = \[[3](#ref-3), [4](#ref-4), [5](#ref-5), [7](#ref-7)\], *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> = 500, *N*<sub><em>g</em></sub> = 99, *θ* = 2.0 and *γ* = 0.10 was used. The total size of the grid coding space is 420 ×420≈ 10<sup><a id="xref-ref-5-23" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a></sup>. In order to capture the inherent randomness and uncertainty present in real-world scenarios, a small amount of neuronal noise was introduced by adding random perturbations to the activation values of hippocampal cells in Vector-HaSH. This noise, generated from a uniform distribution between 0 and 0.1, mimics the fluctuations and disturbances observed in individual neurons.

##### Splitter cells

For [Fig. 7c,g](#F7), we follow an analysis method similar to the analysis done on the experimental data<sup><a id="xref-ref-110-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-110">110</a></sup>. The central stem is divided into 4 equal regions ([Fig. S18b](#F26)), and the mean activation of every hippocampal cell is computed in each of the four regions. [Figure S18c](#F26) plots mean activations in each of the four regions, of cells that show different activity patterns as Vector-HaSH traverses the central stem on Left-Turn and Right-Turn trials. The “activation ratio” on Right-Turn trials versus Left-Turn trials is then calculated for each cell in the region for which the given cell has maximum difference in activations. The distribution of these activation ratios is plotted in [Fig. 7g](#F7), that shows the frequency distribution of cells with preferential firing associated with Left-Turn or Right-Turn trials. Note that the distribution of cells preferring left-turn and right-turn trials is approximately even. The percentage of hippocampal cells with non-differential firing was found to be ≈ 3.896%, and the percentage of hippocampal cells with differential firing was found to be ≈ 96.103% in Vector-HaSH (using a threshold of 2 on the activation ratio).

##### Route encoding

In [Fig. 7d,h](#F7) we employed an ensemble analysis approach mirroring that used in<sup><a id="xref-ref-111-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-111">111</a></sup> to validate if hippocampal cells demonstrate route-dependent activity. Our simulated session comprised four blocks, each representing one of four routes (0-3), with 11 trials per block. We performed ensemble analysis on the maze region common to all routes.

We compared the population vector (PV)—activations of all hippocampal cells on an individual trajectory—to the average activation of these cells across all trajectories on each route (route-PV). Specifically, we compared the PVs for each trajectory to the average activation population vectors (route-PVs) of all four routes, excluding the trajectory in consideration from its route-PV calculation to avoid bias.

Using cosine similarity, we assessed the likeness between each trajectory PV and each of the four route PVs. We then calculated the fraction of correct matches (the highest similarity score was with its corresponding route-PV) and incorrect matches (a higher similarity score was with a different route-PV). The comparison results are shown in [Fig. S16a](#F24), left.

We repeated the process 10,000 times with randomized data to estimate the chance probability of correct matches. We randomized the session data by shuffling trials across blocks, randomly assigning each trajectory to one of the four routes, thereby disrupting any correlation between the hippocampal cell activations and a specific route. [Fig. S16a,right](#F24) depicts a typical result from one such shuffle.

For each matrix element (*i, j*), we plotted the distribution of data from these 10,000 matrices in [Fig. S16b](#F24). We then estimated the Probability Density Function (PDF) from this distribution using a Gaussian kernel (Python’s scipy.stats.gaussian\_kde method). To gauge the chance probability of correct matches in our original, unshuffled analysis, we calculated the percentile position of our observed match proportion, referencing the same matrix element (*i, j*) from the unshuffled matrix in [Fig. S16a](#F24).

[Fig. 7h](#F7) presents the probability of correct matches in the unshuffled analysis based on these distributions from 10,000 shuffles. Low diagonal values indicate that trajectories significantly match only their corresponding route-PVs.

##### Directional cells

For [Figs. 7i,j](#F7) and [Fig. S17](#F25), the directionality index is defined similar to that defined for the experimental data analysis<sup><a id="xref-ref-112-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-112">112</a></sup>,<sup><a id="xref-ref-111-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-111">111</a></sup>. Given the activation (*A*) of a hippocampal cell in positive and negative running directions (*A*<sub>+</sub> and *A*<sub>−</sub>), we define the directionality index as |*A*<sub>+</sub> − *A*<sub>−</sub>| / |*A*<sub>+</sub> + *A*<sub>−</sub>|. By this definition, a directionality index of one indicates activity in one direction only, and a directionality index of zero indicates identical activity in both directions.

We use the same definition of directionality index to compute the directionality of the grid cells in Vector-HaSH, shown in [Fig. S19](#F27).

## A Supplementary Information

This SI is structured as follows: First, we present the quantification metrics and tools used to generate the numerical results presented in this paper in SI Sec. B. Then, in SI Sec. C-D, we provide theoretical guarantees of the results about Vector-HaSH, first in SI Sec. C focusing on the grid-hippocampal memory scaffold and in SI Sec. D focusing on heteroassociative learning with the sensory cells. In particular, in SI Sec. C we prove that the setup of the memory scaffold described in the main text results in a network with an exponentially large number of robust fixed points with large basins of attraction. Then, in SI Sec. D, we first demonstrate that heteroassociative pseudoinverse learning will result in a memory continuum with the desired properties, and then show that one may feasibly replace the pseudoinverse learning with simpler Hebbian learning and continue to obtain qualitatively similar results.

## B Quantification Metrics

### B.1 Software and Data

The source code for the models presented in this paper will be made available at the following GitHub repository upon acceptance:

[https://github.com/FieteLab/](https://github.com/FieteLab/)

### B.2 Mutual Information

In this Appendix, unless otherwise specified, we use ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-33.gif)

to represent the *i*<sup>th</sup> bit of the *μ*<sup>th</sup> pattern stored in the network, and *σ*<sub><em>i</em></sub> to represent the *i*<sup>th</sup> bit of the pattern recovered by the network. Here we primarily consider the case of random patterns such that bits of *s*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> are independently sampled from i.i.d. random variables. This allows us to calculate information theoretic quantities for a single bit, and then scale the calculation by the pattern length to obtain the corresponding quantities for entire patterns.

Further, for simplicity of notation in this section, we overload *σ* and *s* to also represent the random variables from which the stored patterns and recovered patterns are being sampled.

We characterize the quality of pattern recovery by a network through the *mutual information* between stored patterns *s* and recovered patterns *σ*. For discrete random variables, the mutual information can be quantified as: ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-37.gif)

where *H*(*σ*) is the information entropy of the recovered pattern *σ*, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-38.gif)

and *H*(*σ* |*s*) is the conditional entropy of the recovered pattern given the stored pattern *s*, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-39.gif)

As we now show in the following sections, the mutual information can be explicitly computed for dense and sparse random binary patterns.

#### B.2.1 Dense binary patterns

For unbiased random binary {-1,1} patterns, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-40.gif)

Further, since we assumed that each bit is independent, we obtain ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-41.gif)

where *m* is the overlap between the stored and recovered pattern, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-34.gif)

. Using [Eq. (29)](#disp-formula-29), this can be used to obtain ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-42.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-43.gif)

Following [Eq. (27)](#disp-formula-27) we thus obtain ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-44.gif)

#### B.2.2 Sparse binary patterns

For sparse binary {0,1} patterns, let *p* denote the fraction of “1” bits in the stored pattern (i.e., the average activity of the stored pattern). Let the average activity of the recovered pattern be denoted as *q* = ∑<sub><em>i</em></sub> *σ*<sub><em>i</em></sub>/*N*.

Let *P*<sub>1<em>e</em></sub> be the probability of error in a bit of *σ* if the corresponding bit of *s* is 1, and *P*<sub>0<em>e</em></sub> be the error probability in a bit of *σ* if the corresponding bit of *s* is 0. Then, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-45.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-46.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-47.gif)

To obtain the probabilities *P*<sub>1<em>e</em></sub> and *P*<sub>0<em>e</em></sub>, we compute the overlap *m* and the average activity of the recovered pattern *q* in terms of these probabilities as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-48.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-49.gif)

These equations can then be solved to obtain ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-50.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-51.gif)

which can then be used to compute *MI*(*σ* ; *s*) using [Eq. (27)](#disp-formula-27).

#### B.2.3 Continuous random normal patterns

The calculation of mutual information so far has been restricted to the case of discrete binarized patterns. For continuous valued patterns (as in [Fig. 3](#F3)), entropy is ill-defined via [Eq. (28)](#disp-formula-28). Instead, in this case we can defined the differential entropy as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-52.gif)

where *ϕ* (*x*) is the probability density function of the random variable *X*.

For random continuous patterns with patterns are sampled from a normal distribution with zero mean and unit variance, this gives ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-53.gif)

The conditional entropy can similarly be calculated as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-54.gif)

where *r* is the correlation coefficient between *X* and *Y*. This can be used to obtain the mutual information ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-55.gif)

Thus in the case of random normal patterns the mutual information between the stored pattern *s* and the recovered pattern *σ* can be computed directly through the correlation between them using [Equation 44](#disp-formula-45) above.

### B.3 Metrics

We quantify the recovery error, i.e., the error between the stored pattern and the recovered pattern in the network by computing the *L*<sup><a id="xref-ref-2-16" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup> norm of the difference between stored and recovered patterns. This recovery error is then used to quantitatively apply a recovery threshold to ascertain the capacity of the memory scaffold.

After choosing a recovery threshold (see *Methods*), the capacity of the network is defined as the largest number of stored patterns for which the average recovery error across patterns is below threshold.

## C Theoretical Results on the Memory Scaffold

First, we prove that the memory scaffold network has ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-35.gif)

fixed points, while having only ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-36.gif)

synapses, establishing an exponentially large number of fixed points. Then, we demonstrate that each of these basins are maximally large, and finally demonstrate that these basins are convex, ensuring robustness of basins and protection against adversarial input.

### C.1 Justification for the exponentially large capacity of the memory scaffold

We first provide broad qualitative justification for why the memory scaffold as constructed in Vector-HaSH is capable of storing such a large number of fixed points, then present a mathematical proof in a simplified setting. In this subsection, for ease of notation, we denote the number of phases in the *i*<sup>th</sup> grid module, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-37.gif)

, as *l*<sub><em>i</em></sub>.

Unlike associative memory in the usual context of random patterns (as in the random shuffled hippocampal states considered in [Figs. 2d,f](#F2) and [3d](#F3)), note that the hippocampal states are determined by a random projection of the structured grid states. As a result, the predefined hippocampal states inherit similar pattern-pattern correlations as the predefined grid cell states. This allows for Hebbian learning to act more efficiently in learning pairwise correlation resulting in a high capacity. Indeed, while in Hopfield networks any given fixed point is destabilized due to interference from other fixed points (resulting in catastrophic forgetting when a large number of fixed points have been memorized), the shared pattern-pattern correlations in the memory scaffold result in the interference terms being positively correlated with each fixed point (which also leads to the scaffold generalization properties [Fig. 2f](#F2), Sec. C.4).

To show this result more quantitatively, recall that ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-56.gif)

where CAN\[*x*\] is a nonlinear function that acts independently on each module of grid cells, such that CAN\[*x*\] will be a vector with exactly one element set to “1” in each of the *l*<sub><em>i</em></sub> indices corresponding to each module. and all other elements set to “0”. Further, the element set to 1 in a given module corresponds to the same index as the largest element *x* within that module.

Corresponding to the state *h*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>, consider the pattern *h*(*t*) = *h*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> + *ζ*, where *ζ* represents a random noise vector. For simplicity, we assume that *ζ* is a continuous-valued vector whose each component is drawn independently from a normal distribution with zero mean and variance *ε*<sup><a id="xref-ref-2-17" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup>.

From *h*(*t*), we aim to recover *g*(*t* + 1) = *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> via the mapping *W*<sub><em>gh</em></sub>. For ease of notation, we denote the prespecified random projection *W*<sub><em>hg</em></sub> as *W* . ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-57.gif)

where Φ represents the neural transfer function for the grid to hippocampal synapses, which we implement as a thresholded rectifying function (see *Methods*). We implement *W* such that each element is independently sampled from a prespecified distribution (see *Methods*). Without loss of generality, we can assume that this distribution has zero mean and unit variance, since any transformations of the mean and variance can be absorbed into the nonlinearity Φ.

Now, from the definition of *W*<sub><em>gh</em></sub> and *h*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-58.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-59.gif)

where we have added a scaling factor 1/*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> that leaves the CAN continuous attractor dynamics unchanged, but will be useful for normalization of random variables later in our calculation.

For analytic simplicity, we make the assumption that the nonlinearity Φ in the above equation can be ignored. While this is a gross simplification, the obtained results are broadly consistent with the numerical observations in [Fig. 2](#F2). This approximates the above equation to ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-60.gif)

where *A* = (*GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup>)(*W*<sup><em>T</em></sup>*W*/*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>), and *Z* = *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup>*W*<sup><em>T</em></sup> *ζ* /*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>.

Since each element of the *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> × *N*<sub><em>g</em></sub> matrix *W* was drawn independently from a normal distribution with unit variance, *W*<sup><em>T</em></sup>*W* (and hence *A*) can be treated as a matrix random variable. Under the distribution of the matrix random variable *A* and the vector random variable *Z* we will compute the probability of *g*(*t* + 1) = *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>. Note that this simplification of the problem into [Eq. (49)](#disp-formula-50) has fundamentally relied on [Eq. (46)](#disp-formula-47), which establishes hippocampal states as being derived from random projections of grid states. Qualitatively, hippocampal states being projections of grid states results in a similarity of state-state relationships between grid states and hippocampal states. As a result, overloading the scaffold network weights with a large number of patterns will not result in loss of previously stored information through interference; instead, pattern interference will re-inforce previously stored patterns (which also results in the strong generalization property, Sec. C.4). In contrast, if the hippocampal states were arbitrarily determined (such as through consideration of random sparse vectors, or sensory-input-dependent vectors), then interference due to storage of additional patterns would result in catastrophic forgetting, as in classic Hopfield memory.

We first focus on the structure of the matrix *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup>. This matrix will have a block structure, with the sizes of the blocks determined by the number of phases in each grid module, *l*<sub><em>i</em></sub>. In particular we write *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup> as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-61.gif)

with each Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub> being a submatrix of size *l*<sub><em>i</em></sub> × *l* <sub><em>j</em></sub>. From the structure of the grid code matrix *G*, it follows that ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-62.gif)

and ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-63.gif)

where 𝕀 is an appropriately sized identity matrix, and **1** is an appropriately sized matrix with each element equal to 1. This can be shown by noting that *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup> = ∑<sub><em>μ</em></sub> *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> (*g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>)<sup><em>T</em></sup>, and that each *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> (*g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>)<sup><em>T</em></sup> will be a matrix with a single nonzero element equal to 1 in each Γ block of *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup>.

We now compute the distribution of the matrix random variable *W*<sup><em>T</em></sup>*W*. As argued above, each element of the *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> × *N*<sub><em>g</em></sub> matrix *W* can be assumed to be drawn independently from a distribution with zero mean and unit variance. As is justified later, we can assume that these elements are drawn from a normal distribution in particular, since we shall be applying central limit theorem which will wash away particulars of the shape of the distribution.

Thus *W*<sup><em>T</em></sup>*W* can thus be approximated to have each diagonal element distributed as the sum of the squares of *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> standard normal variables, and each off-diagonal element distributed as the sum of the products of *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> pairs of uncorrelated standard normal variables. Thus ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-64.gif)

where *χ*<sup><a id="xref-ref-2-18" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup>(*N*) is the sum of *N* i.i.d. *χ*<sup><a id="xref-ref-2-19" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup> distributions, and 𝒩 𝒫 (*N*) is the sum of *N* i.i.d. normal product distributions (i.e., the distribution of the product of two i.i.d. standard normal variables). Note that we have suppressed the indices on each matrix element, however it should be noted that each element is an independent sample from the distribution and are identical in distribution but not in value.

In the large *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> limit, each of these matrix elements is the sum of a large number of random variables and can hence be approximated as a normal distribution due to central limit theorem. Thus, *χ*<sup><a id="xref-ref-2-20" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup>(*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>) ∼ 𝒩 (*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>, 2*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>), and 𝒩 𝒫 (*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>) ∼ 𝒩 (0, *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>), where 𝒩 (*μ, σ* <sup><a id="xref-ref-2-21" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup>) is a normal distribution with mean *μ* and variance *σ* <sup><a id="xref-ref-2-22" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup>.<sup><a id="xref-ref-3-15" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a></sup>

We thus treat *W*<sup><em>T</em></sup>*W* /*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> as a matrix random variable with elements on the diagonal being drawn from a distribution 𝒟, having unit mean and a variance of 2/*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>; and elements on the off-diagonal being drawn from a distribution 𝒪, having zero mean and 1/*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> variance. For ease of calculation, we write this matrix as having a block structure similar to *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup>, given by ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-65.gif)

with *w*<sub><em>i j</em></sub> being an *l*<sub><em>i</em></sub> ×*l* <sub><em>j</em></sub> matrix such that *w*<sub><em>ii</em></sub> has diagonal entries drawn from 𝒟 and off diagonal entries drawn from 𝒪, and *w*<sub><em>i j</em></sub> for *i* ≠ *j* being a matrix with all entries drawn from 𝒪

We can now compute the distribution of the elements of *A*. The matrix *A* will have a similar block structure to *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup>, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-66.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-67.gif)

with ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-68.gif)

Since each *w*<sub><em>i j</em></sub> consists of elements drawn from random normal distributions, the distribution of the matrix variables *A*<sub><em>i j</em></sub> can be exactly computed through sums of random normal variables. Even without explicit computation, we can ascertain certain properties of *A* given the symmetry of grid states across module-preserving permutations. In particular, *A*<sub><em>ii</em></sub> will be a matrix random variable with diagonal element drawn from an i.i.d. distribution ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-38.gif)

, and each off-diagonal element drawn from a different i.i.d. distribution ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-39.gif)

. In contrast, *A*<sub><em>i j</em></sub> for *i*≠ *j* will have all elements drawn from an i.i.d. distribution 𝒜<sub><em>i j</em></sub>.

We first consider *A*<sub><em>ii</em></sub>. ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-69.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-70.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-71.gif)

where we have omitted subscripts on individual random variables for simplicity, but it should be noted that each random variable is i.i.d., including the summands in the above expressions. Thus, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-72.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-73.gif)

A similar calculation can be done to obtain ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-74.gif)

From the same symmetry as in *A*, we can also argue that elements of *Z* can also be split into a similar block structure, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-40.gif)

, with all ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-41.gif)

elements of *Z*<sub><em>i</em></sub> drawn from an i.i.d distribution Ƶ<sub><em>i</em></sub>. More specifically, *Z* = *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup>*W*<sup><em>T</em></sup> *ζ* /*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>. First note that *W*<sup><em>T</em></sup> *ζ* will be a random vector with each element constructed from the sum of *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> i.i.d. normal product distributions multiplied by the scale of *ζ*, i.e., *ε*. Thus *W*<sup><em>T</em></sup> *ζ* /*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> is identically distributed to *ε*𝒪. Left multiplying this vector with *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup> we obtain in the *i*<sup>th</sup> subvector ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-75.gif)

where again we have omitted subscripts on individual random variables for simplicity.

Let the mean and standard deviation of ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

and Ƶ<sub><em>i</em></sub> be denoted as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

Next, we note the conditions on these blocks to make *g*(*t* + 1) = *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>, the condition necessary for *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> to be a scaffold fixed point. Without loss of generality, we assume that *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> corresponds to the grid state such that each *l*<sub><em>i</em></sub> length subvector of *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> has the first element set to 1 and all others set to zero. The *i*<sup>th</sup> subvector of *Ag*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> + *Z* will then have the first element given by ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-84.gif)

and all other elements given by ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-85.gif)

For this module to be correctly reconstructed through the continuous attractor network dynamics, we require that the first element of the subvector to be larger than the others. Thus, the probability of the correct reconstruction is given by ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-86.gif)

As seen earlier, each of these random variables are being drawn from a normal distribution (due to central limit theorem in the limit of large *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>). In terms of the parameters of these normal distributions, [Eq. (75)](#disp-formula-76) can be written as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-87.gif)

For ease of notation, we define ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-88.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-89.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-90.gif)

such that the right-hand side of [Eq. (76)](#disp-formula-77) is equal to ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-45.gif)

. This can then be computed as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-91.gif)

The above-derived expressions for the terms in *μ*<sub><em>AZ</em></sub> and *σ*<sub><em>AZ</em></sub> can be simplified to ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-92.gif)

and ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-93.gif)

Recall that *γ*<sub><em>ii</em></sub> = *P*/*l*<sub><em>i</em></sub> and *γ*<sub><em>i j</em></sub> = *P*/(*l*<sub><em>i</em></sub>*l* <sub><em>j</em></sub>), for *P* = ∏<sub><em>i</em></sub> *l*<sub><em>i</em></sub>. Thus, the ratio ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-46.gif)

simplifies to ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-94.gif)

Inverting the obtained expression allows for computation of ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-47.gif)

, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-95.gif)

where *c* = 2 erf<sup>−1</sup>(1 2*P*) <sup><a id="xref-ref-2-23" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup> and *P* is the threshold selected for accuracy of the recovered pattern. This allows us to estimate the critical number of hippocampal cells (as a function of the number of grid cell modules, *M*, the period of the modules ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-48.gif)

,and the input noise *ε*) beyond which the hippocampal memory scaffold stores all grid-hippocampal states as fixed points.

If the number of grid cells far exceeds the number of modules (as would be expected<sup><a id="xref-ref-81-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">81</a></sup>), then *λ*<sub><em>k</em></sub> ≫ *M* and thus *l*<sub><em>k</em></sub> ≫ *M*<sub>2</sub> and the summands in [Eq. (84)](#disp-formula-85) can all be ignored. This makes ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-49.gif)

asymptotically independent of the grid periods, and is given by ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-50.gif)

. This can be seen qualitatively in [Fig. 2](#F2), where for a fixed *M*, the critical number of hippocampal cells ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-51.gif)

approaches a constant with increasing *N*<sub><em>g</em></sub> (and hence increasing *l*<sub><em>k</em></sub>). Moreover, if *ε* ≪1 and *M*≫ 1, then ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-52.gif)

. This has been verified qualitatively in [Fig. 2](#F2), where ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-53.gif)

increases linearly with increasing number of modules *M*. Note that due to the simplifications necessary for this analytic result, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-54.gif)

obtained from [Eq. (84)](#disp-formula-85) are not directly comparable to numerics in [Fig. 2](#F2), however the above-mentioned qualitative trends all seem to hold.

These results thus demonstrate a crucial property of the hippocampal memory scaffold network — it has ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-55.gif)

synapses while having ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-56.gif)

fixed points. Thus, the number of fixed points grows exponentially faster than the number of synapses in the network, resulting in the network being useful as a memory scaffold as in MESH<sup><a id="xref-ref-87-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">87</a></sup>.

### C.2 Memory Scaffold has Maximally Sized Basins of Attraction

Due to the symmetries in grid code, we argue here that the memory scaffold in Vector-HaSH has no spurious fixed points, and has convex, maximally sized basins of attraction that are equal in volume.

First, we note that as a result of the CAN dynamics in the grid layer (cf. [Eq. (4)](#disp-formula-4)), the only possible grid states are the ∏<sub><em>i</em></sub> *K*<sub><em>i</em></sub> modular one-hot states. Correspondingly, the hippocampal states (determined by random projections of the grid states) must then also be one of the ∏<sub><em>i</em></sub> *K*<sub><em>i</em></sub> states, establishing that no spurious fixed points can arise.

Thus, the union of the basins about each of the predefined fixed points of the grid-hippocampal scaffold cover the entire space ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-57.gif)

. Note next that each *h*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> are equivalent, i.e., there is no special *μ* since each *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> is equivalent up to a module-preserving permutation of bits and *h*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> are determined by a random projection of *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>. Thus, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-58.gif)

must be partitioned into basins with equal volume that are maximally large (and hence are of the same volume as the Voronoi cell about these fixed points).

### C.3 Convexity of Scaffold Basins

The existence of maximally sized equi-volumed basins around each predefined scaffold fixed point, as we have shown, is not sufficient to guarantee robustness to noise. A large basin could in principle have some boundaries that come arbitrarily close to the fixed points – such a situation holds for instance when a system is susceptible to adversarial inputs, where a very small perturbation of the input leads to a very different classification as an output. Noise robustness requires a second condition, that of basin convexity. Here we demonstrate that the obtained basins are convex, and thus the large basins must result in basin boundaries that are well separated from the fixed points themselves.

We are interested in the basins in the space![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-59.gif)

: the hippocampus receives sensory input from the sensory layer, mediating the recall of scaffold states. Thus, noise robustness will hence be required there. The broad idea of the proof is as follows: first, we demonstrate that perturbations in the hippocampal latent space are equivalent to considering real-valued perturbations with small magnitudes in the grid-cell layer latent space. Then we show that the continuous attractor dynamics on grid cells result in convex basins in the grid-cell space, which directly translates to convex basins in the hippocampal space.

Consider a hippocampal population vector given by a small perturbation to a predefined hippocampal state fixed point *h*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>, which we denote as *h* = *h*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> + *ε*. Let *δ* denote the magnitude of the perturbation *ε*. This hippocampal state is projected onto the grid cells through *W*<sub><em>gh</em></sub> to obtain ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-60.gif)

before the continuous attractor dynamics, where ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-96.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-97.gif)

Note that *W*<sub><em>gh</em></sub>*ε* will have a magnitude of approximately *δ* times the magnitude of ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-61.gif)

, and further, the nonzero elements of *ε* are uncorrelated with *h*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>, and hence *W*<sub><em>gh</em></sub>*ε* can be treated as an independent small real-valued perturbation to ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-62.gif)

.

If we can now show that the continuous attractor dynamics on grid cells has a convex basin, that would indicate that all points near *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> map to *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>, and since points near *h*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> map to points near *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>, this would imply convexity of basins in *p*\-space.

The symmetry of the grid code implies that it will suffice to show that the basin about any one fixed point is convex. Without loss of generality, we choose the fixed point *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> as the grid population vector with the first bit in each module set to 1 and all other ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-63.gif)

bits set to 0. Let *x* and *y* be two vectors within the continuous attractor dynamics of *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>, i.e., *CAN*\[*x*\] = *CAN* \[*y*\] = *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>. Thus, for the *k*<sup>th</sup> module, *x* <sup><em>k</em>;1</sup> > *x*<sup><em>k,I</em></sup> and *y* <sup><em>k</em>;1</sup> > *y*<sup><em>k,I</em></sup> for *i* > 1. Adding the two inequalities with coefficients *a* and (1 −*a*), we obtain *ax*<sub><em>k</em>;1</sub> + (1 −*a*)*y*<sub><em>k</em>,1</sub> > *ax*<sub><em>k</em>;<em>i</em></sub> + (1 −*a*)*y*<sub><em>k,i</em></sub> for all *i* > 1 for 0 ≤*a*≤1. Thus, continuous attractor dynamics (which enforce modular winner-take-all dynamics) map the *k*<sup>th</sup> module of *ax* + (1 −*a*)*y* to the *k*<sup>th</sup> module of *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>. Since this holds for all *k*, thus CAN\[*ax* + (1 −*a*)*y*\] = *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>. Hence, for any two vectors *x* and *y* in the basin of *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>, all vectors on the line from *x* to *y* also lie in this basin. By definition, this makes the basin of *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>, and as argued earlier this imposes convexity of basins in the hippocampal cell space.

### C.4 Scaffold weights can be learned on a vanishing fraction of all states

As shown in [Fig. 2](#F2), when the hippocampus-to-grid cell synaptic weights (*W*<sub><em>gh</em></sub>) are learned on a small number of grid states, the scaffold is able to generalize: all grid states become stable fixed points of the scaffold dynamics. Thus an animal only needs to traverse small regions in space after which the grid-hippocampal scaffold is recurrently stabilized for all other states. Here we show that in the large *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> limit, it will suffice to train on only ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-64.gif)

patterns for stabilization of the complete scaffold, where *λ*<sub><em>max</em></sub> is the largest period of any grid module.

Similar to Sec. C.1, we make the grossly simplified assumption that the nonlinearities in the hippocampus can be ignored. The grid cell state would then evolve as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-98.gif)

where again we add an 1/*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> scaling factor that renders the continuous attractor network dynamics unchanged. For *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> to be a fixed point of the recurrent scaffold dynamics, we thus require that *g*(*t* + 1) be equal to *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>. Unlike Sec. C.1, we assume here that *G* is the matrix constructed by appending grid cell population vectors over only the first *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> number of states (rather than over the entirety of scaffold states).

As argued earlier in Sec. C.1, for large *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>, the matrix *W*<sup><em>T</em></sup>*W* /*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> can be considered to be a random variable with i.i.d 𝒩 (1, 2/*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>) random variables on the diagonal, and i.i.d 𝒩 (0, 1/*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>) entries on the off-diagonal. In the limit of *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> → ∞, these distributions tend to Dirac delta distributions we can thus treat *W*<sup><em>T</em></sup>*W* /*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> as simply being an identity matrix. Thus, in this limit, it suffices to examine the scaffold fixed points under the dynamics ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-99.gif)

As earlier, we write *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup> as a block matrix ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-100.gif)

with each Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub> being a submatrix of size ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-65.gif)

. We define a construct a sequence of patterns *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> as follows: let the first pattern *g*<sup><a id="xref-ref-1-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a></sup> be such that the first element in each ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-66.gif)

subvector is set to one, and all other elements set to zero. Then, each successive pattern shifts the active element by one, modulo the total number of elements in the subvector ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-67.gif)

. Mapped to real space, this corresponds to the sequence of locations shown in [Fig. S12a](#F20) (*top left*). As we will now show, setting *W*<sub><em>gh</em></sub> based on only ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-68.gif)

grid patterns will suffice to stabilize all patterns.

Note that ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-101.gif)

Each *μ* term of this summation, *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> (*g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>)<sup><em>T</em></sup> will be a matrix with exactly one ‘1’ in each block Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub>, at the location (*μ* mod ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-69.gif)

mod ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-70.gif)

), and will be zero everywhere else.

For *i* ≠*j*, the periods *λ*<sub><em>i</em></sub> and *λ*<sub><em>j</em></sub> are coprime. In this case, we can see that if *g* and *g* contribute a ‘1’ at the same location (*m, n*) in Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub> then *μ* − *ν* must be a multiple of ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-71.gif)

. This can be seen since *m* = *μ* mod ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-72.gif)

, thus *μ* = *m* mod ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-73.gif)

. Similarly *ν* = *m* mod ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-74.gif)

, implying that *μ* − *ν* = 0 mod ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-75.gif)

. Similar reasoning leads to *μ* − *ν* = 0 mod ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-76.gif)

and thus *μ* − *ν* = 0 mod ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-77.gif)

. Crucially, this means that if *μ* ≠ *ν*, then *μ* − *ν* must be at least ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-78.gif)

, which is equal to the number of elements in Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub>.

Thus, if both *μ* and *ν* contribute a ‘1’ to (*m, n*) in Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub>, it must be that all other elements in Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub> have been increased by 1 due to patterns between *μ* and *ν*. In essence, elements of Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub> increase sequentially through increasing terms in the summation [Eq. (90)](#disp-formula-91). Starting from all elements at zero before any learning, all patterns increase to 1 one-at-a-time up to ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-79.gif)

, all patterns increase up to 2 through the next ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-80.gif)

patterns and so on. Crucially, at any point during learning, the largest element of Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub>, which we denote max Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub>, can differ from the smallest element of Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub>, which we denote min Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub>, by at most 1.

Next, we observe that for Γ<sub><em>ii</em></sub>, the pattern *μ* contributes a ‘1’ at the location (*μ* mod ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-81.gif)

, *μ* mod ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-82.gif)

). This leads to two observations: first, that Γ<sub><em>ii</em></sub> will have nonzero entries only on its diagonal; second, the smallest element on the diagonal will be ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-83.gif)

and the largest element on the diagonal will be ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-84.gif)

.

Now, consider the matrix *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup> constructed using [Eq. (90)](#disp-formula-91), trained through the first *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> patterns. We apply [Eq. (88)](#disp-formula-89) for a given *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup>, for *μ* that need not be within {1 · · ·, *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub>}. Note *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> has a 1 at only one location per module. Thus the *i*<sup>th</sup> subvector of *GG*<sup><em>T</em></sup> *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> can have values as small as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-85.gif)

min Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub> at the index where *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> equals 1 in the *i*<sup>th</sup> module; and, it can have values as large as ∑ <sub><em>j</em>≠<em>i</em></sub> max Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub> at the other entries. For this subvector to map to *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> under the continuous attractor network dynamics, we thus require ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-102.gif)

Thus, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-103.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-104.gif)

Thus ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-86.gif)

. Since the correct subvector needs to be recovered for all modules, thus ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-87.gif)

for stabilization of *all* grid states *g*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> in the large *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> limit.

Note that the proof above relies on the particular ordering of grid and hippocampal states described above. As we demonstrate, this ordering is optimal, and no other ordering of grid states can result in ‘faster’ generalization to all scaffold fixed points. To see this, note that we showed above that the particular ordering choice made ensures that the largest and smallest elements of Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub> can differ by at most 1. Moreover, this difference of at most one resulted in the generalization result proved above. Correspondingly, any other ordering that maintains this difference between the largest and smallest elements of Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub> will also demonstrate generalization to all scaffold states at ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-88.gif)

. Faster generalization could only be possible if the elements of Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub> we all identical, leading to a difference of zero. This is however impossible, since the sum of elements in Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub> is equal to *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub>, which is increasing in steps of 1 and is thus not always divisible by the number of entries in Γ<sub><em>ij</em></sub> i.e., ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-89.gif)

. Conversely, any other ordering, will result in a potentially larger difference between the smallest and largest elements of Γ<sub><em>i j</em></sub>, which (following [Eq. 92](#disp-formula-93)) will thus require a larger number of patterns to generalize to all scaffold states.

However, as noted in [Fig. 2g](#F2), other contiguous orderings of grid states result in generalization upon learning an approximately similar number ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-90.gif)

of patterns.

## D Theoretical Results on Heteroassociative Learning

Here we demonstrate that pseudoinverse learning first perfectly recovers the hippocampal states provided that *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> > *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> (in the noise-free case). Following the memory scaffold results proven earlier, reconstruction of the correct hippocampal states then results in correct retrieval of the corresponding label layer states. Next, we prove that for *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> < *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>, the reconstructed feature layer states are also perfectly reconstructed, and for larger *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> the overlap of the stored and recovered patterns decays gracefully as described in the main text. We then prove that given an ideal memory scaffold, heteroassociative *Hebbian* learning is also sufficient to obtain a memory scaffold with the same qualitative properties, with only a smaller prefactor on the memory capacity.

### D.1 Perfect Reconstruction of Hippocampal States Through Heteroassociative Pseudoinverse Learning

The projection of the learned sensory inputs onto the hippocampus is given by *W*<sub><em>hs</em></sub>*S* = *HS*<sup>+</sup>*S* = *H*Λ<sub><em>S</em></sub>, where Λ<sub><em>S</em></sub> = *S*<sup>+</sup>*S* is an orthogonal projection operator onto the range of *S*<sup><em>T</em></sup>. If *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> ≥ *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub>, *S* has linearly independent columns, and Λ<sub><em>S</em></sub> = 𝕀, the identity matrix. Thus, *W*<sub><em>hs</em></sub>*S* = *H*, i.e., cuing any memorized patterns results in accurate reconstruction of the corresponding hippocampal scaffold state

However, to examine Vector-HaSH as an associative memory, it is necessary to examine to reconstruction of the correct hippocampal scaffold state when cued with noisy or corrupted versions of the memorized patterns as well. Following the results of SI Sec. C.3 and C.2, we see that the memory scaffold has maximally large convex basins of attraction. Note that once *W*<sub><em>hs</em></sub> has been trained with pseuodo-inverse learning, the mapping from the sensory layer to the hippocampal layer is simply a linear transformation, that maps stored sensory patterns to their corresponding hippocampal scaffold states. Thus, the regions in sensory space that map to a given scaffold state must simply be a lower-to-higher-dimensional linear transformation of convex basins in the scaffold space about the chosen state.

Hence, the basins of attraction for a given scaffold state must be convex regions in the sensory input state that include the sensory pattern that has been associated with that scaffold state.

### D.2 Perfect Reconstruction of *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> Sensory States Through Heteroassociative Pseudoinverse Learning

To show that up to *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> ≤ *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> sensory inputs can be perfectly reconstructed through Vector-HaSH, we require that the matrix of fixed point hippocampal states *H* be strongly full rank. While we do not rigorously prove that *H* is full rank, we provide intuitive justification for the same. First note that rank(*W*<sub><em>hg</em></sub>*G*) = rank(*G*) = *N*<sub><em>G</em></sub> −*M* + 1<sup><a id="xref-ref-136-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-136">136</a></sup>, which does not need to be as large as *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>.

Applying a thresholded rectifying function, *H* = ReLU\[*W*<sub><em>hg</em></sub>*G* −Θ\], effectively acts as an independent random perturbation to the elements of *H*. Assuming that these perturbations are truly random, *H* (and submatrices of *H* formed by selecting varied numbers of fixed points) will become full rank. This is numerically verified in [Fig. S10](#F18), where the rank can be seen to be min(*N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub>, *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>).

We can now show that the “knee” of the Vector-HaSH memory continuum must be at *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>, with *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> ≤ *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> sensory states being perfectly reconstructed.

The projection of the hippocampus states onto the sensory layer is given by *W*<sub><em>sh</em></sub>*H* = *SH*<sup>+</sup>*H* = *S*Λ<sub><em>H</em></sub>, where Λ<sub><em>H</em></sub> = *H*<sup>+</sup>*H* is an orthogonal projection operator onto the range of *H*<sup><em>T</em></sup>. Since *H* is strongly full rank (as justified above), thus for up to *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> ≤ *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>, the projection operator Λ<sub><em>H</em></sub> will equal 𝕀, the identity matrix. Thus *W*<sub><em>sh</em></sub>*P* = *S*.

Instead, Λ<sub><em>H</em></sub> projects on to the *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>\-dimensional hyperplane 𝒫<sub><em>H</em></sub> spanned by the rows of *H*. Notationally, let ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

be the vector corresponding to the *i*<sup>th</sup> row of ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

By construction *s*<sub><em>i</em></sub> are *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub>\-dimensional random vectors with no privileged direction. Thus, *s*<sub><em>i</em></sub>, the squared magnitude along each dimension, will on average be equally divided across all dimensions. Hence, on average, theJcomponent of *s*<sub><em>i</em></sub> projected onto 𝒫<sub><em>H</em></sub> (i.e., ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

) will have a squared magnitude of *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>|*s*<sub><em>i</em></sub>|<sup><a id="xref-ref-2-24" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup>/*N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> and thus ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)
