![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-104.gif)

is also the cosine of the angle between *s*<sub><em>i</em></sub> and the hyperplane 𝒫<sub><em>H</em></sub>, and hence averaged over *i*, ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-105.gif)

Note that ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-105.gif)

, and ∑<sub><em>i</em></sub> |*s*<sub><em>i</em></sub>|<sup><a id="xref-ref-2-25" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup> = ∑<sub><em>μ</em></sub> |*s*<sup><em>μ</em></sup> |<sup><a id="xref-ref-2-26" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup>. Thus the above equation can be rewritten as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-106.gif)

where ⟨⟩<sub><em>μ</em></sub> denotes an average over all patterns *μ*.

In the notation of [Eq. 33](#disp-formula-34), this gives ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-106.gif)

<sub><em>s</em></sub>. In the limit of small *m*, note that log(1 + *m*) ≈ *m*, and the right-hand side of [Eq. 33](#disp-formula-34) can simply be approximated in the asymptotic limit as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-107.gif)

and thus mutual information scales as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-108.gif)

As a consequence of this result, note that since the mutual information is always positive and only smoothly degrades 1 with increasing *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub>, thus the recovered state only gradually moves away from the true pattern in sensory space (cf. [Fig. 3g](#F3)). For random uncorrelated sensory patterns, the boundary of the Voronoi cell about a memorized pattern corresponds to the boundary at which no information is being recovered specific to one particular pattern. Thus, an always positive mutual information indicates that the recovered pattern always remains with the Voronoi cell corresponding to the true pattern, i.e., the recovered pattern is always closer to the correct patterns as compared to any other pattern.

### D.4 Space- and time-complexity of memory in Vector-HaSH

We show in SI Sec. D.1 that the number of sensory cells can scale as fast or faster than the maximal scaffold capacity 𝒪 (*K*<sup><em>M</em></sup>). Thus, *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> ≫*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>, *N*<sub><em>g</em></sub>, and thus the number of synapses in the model, # synapses = 2*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>(*N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> + *N*<sub><em>g</em></sub>) + *M* ∗*K*<sup><a id="xref-ref-2-27" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a></sup> is dominated by 2*N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> ∗*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>. Moreover, the number of hippocampal cells is asymptotically constant for large *K* at a fixed *M* ([Fig. 2d](#F2) *right*, SI [Fig. S2](#F10)), and thus the number of synapses scales as 𝒪 (*N*<sub><em>s</em></sub>). Further, the number of nodes, *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> + *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> + *N*<sub><em>g</em></sub> also scales as 𝒪 (*N*<sub><em>s</em></sub>). Since the number of patterns perfectly reconstructed *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> is constant (with respect to the asymptotic scaling of the number of synapses at a fixed *M*), the continuum in Vector-HaSH ranges from storing 𝒪)(1) patterns with 𝒪 (*N*<sub><em>s</em></sub>) = 𝒪 (# synapses) each, up to storing 𝒪 (*N*<sub><em>s</em></sub>) = 𝒪 (scaffold size) patterns with positive recovered information.

The memory storage requirement for Vector-HaSH is equal to the number of synapses in the model, which as noted above scales as 𝒪 (*N*<sub><em>s</em></sub>*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>). This permits storage of *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> patterns perfectly, consisting of a total of *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub>*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> bits of information. Thus, as in Hopfield and Hopfield-like networks, the total information stored and recalled in Vector-HaSH scales as the number of synapses. The time complexity for perfect recovery of all 𝒪 (*N*<sub><em>s</em></sub>*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>) = 𝒪 (# synapses) bits of information scales as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-107.gif)

. In contrast, for Hopfield and Hopfield-like networks, the time complexity for recovery of 𝒪 (# synapses) scales as 𝒪 ((# synapses)<sup>3/2</sup>). Vector-HaSH thus has an asympototically faster time complexity than Hopfield-like networks for recovery for the same number of total bits of information (when normalized by the number of synapses in the model).

When the number of patterns stored is larger and scales with the number of sensory cells, *cN*<sub><em>s</em></sub> for 0 < *c*≤ 1, Vector-HaSH partially recovers the stored information ([Fig. 3](#F3)). In this regime, Vector-HaSH has additionally improved time and space complexity as compared with the number of synapses: a space requirement of only (# synapses) and a time complexity of only ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-108.gif)

is needed to store an input of 𝒪 (*c*(# synapses) × (*N*<sub><em>s</em></sub>/*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>)) bits of information.

The above scalings can also be reinterpreted in terms of the total information, *I*, stored in the networks (where for perfect recovery *I* = *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>*N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> = # synapses, and for partial recovery ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-109.gif)

. For perfect recovery the space complexity requirements scale as *I*, and time complexity scales as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-110.gif)

. For partial recovery the space complexity scales as *IN*<sub><em>h</em></sub>/(*cN*<sub><em>s</em></sub>), and time complexity scales as ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-111.gif)

..

### D.5 One-Step Heteroassociation Leads to a Memory Continuum even with Hebbian Learning

The memory continuum in Vector-HaSH is a result of the one-step heteroassociation from the hidden to the feature layer, given a memory scaffold that perfectly recovers the hidden states. This holds irrespective of the nature of heteroassociation (pseudoinverse learning or Hebbian learning).

Here we consider a simpler scenario where *W*<sub><em>sh</em></sub> is trained through Hebbian learning and the hippocampal states are assumed to be correctly reconstructed (corresponding to pseudoinverse learning from *S* to *H*).

We assume here that the sensory patterns being stored are random unbiased binary vectors drawn uniformly from ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-112.gif)

. If the weights from the hippocampal cells to the sensory inputs, *W*<sub><em>sh</em></sub> are trained using Hebbian learning: ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-109.gif)

To evaluate the accuracy of a recovered sensory state through this weight matrix, we estimate the probability that the *i*<sup>th</sup> bit of *s*<sup><em>ν</em></sup> is recovered correctly. Since we assume in this simplified scenario that the hippocampal state *p*<sup><em>ν</em></sup> has been correctly recovered: ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-110.gif)

Here we have separated the pattern *ν* from all the other patterns. Next, we multiply the second term on the right-hand side by a factor ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-113.gif)

, and pull ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-114.gif)

out of the argument of the sign-function since ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-115.gif)

. ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-111.gif)

where ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-112.gif)

Successful recovery of the *i*<sup>th</sup> bit of *s*<sub><em>ν</em></sub> will occur if ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-116.gif)

, which holds provided that *a*<sub><em>iν</em></sub> > 1. The probability of an error in a given randomly chosen bit can thus be calculated as the probabilty that *a*<sub><em>iν</em></sub> > 1.

Since the sensory states were assumed to have been drawn uniformly from ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-117.gif)

, we can treat the product ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

as being +1 or −1 with equal probability. We assume that the distribution of hippocampal cell activity has mean *μ*<sub><em>p</em></sub> and variance ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-119.gif)

. Further, assuming that ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-120.gif)

and ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-121.gif)

can be treated as independent random variables, the product ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-122.gif)

would then have mean ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-123.gif)

and variance ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-124.gif)

. Accounting for the random sign introduced by ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-125.gif)

, the summand in [Eq. (100)](#disp-formula-102) can then be treated as a random variable *X* with mean ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-126.gif)

and variance ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-127.gif)

with probability 0.5, and mean ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-128.gif)

and variance ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-129.gif)

with probability 0.5. For large *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> and *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub>, we are summing over a large number of random variables — thus due to the Central Limit theorem, the precise details of the distribution will not matter, apart from an estimate of its mean and variance. By symmetry, the mean of *X* will be zero. This variance can be calculated to be ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-130.gif)

.

Summing over (*N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> −1)*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> ≈*N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub>*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> terms in [Eq. (100)](#disp-formula-102), evaluated through central limit theorem, thus gives a normal distribution, with zero mean, and variance ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/inline-graphic-131.gif)

. This gives ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-113.gif)

with ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-114.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-115.gif)

![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-116.gif)

The probability of error in the activity state of neuron *i* is therefore given by: ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-117.gif)

Thus the probability of error increases with the ratio *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub>/*N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>. The mutual information between the stored and recovered Sensory States is then: ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-118.gif)

Since erf(*x*) ≈*x* and *log*(1 + *x*) ≈*x* for small *x*, in the limit of a large *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> the above expression can be approximated to ![Embedded Image](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Embedded Image](https://www.biorxiv.org/sites/default/files/highwire/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/embed/graphic-119.gif)

This asymptotic scaling is verified in [Fig. S6](#F14)

### D.6 Pseudoinverse learning of mappings from sensory states to velocities and memory palace items

Similar to pseudoinverse learning done from hippocampal cells to sensory cells, an exactly equivalent mathematical theory applies for pseudoinverse learning from sensory cells to the one-hot representation of velocities associated with each sensory state in 6 (see *Methods* for details of the learned velocity representations). In particular, as seen in Sec. D.2, pseudoinverse learning of a matrix *W*<sub><em>yx</em></sub> that maps from a layer *X* of dimensionality *N*<sub><em>x</em></sub> to a layer *Y* of dimensionality *N*<sub><em>y</em></sub> is successful in exact recovery of all patterns in layer *Y* when learning up to rank(*X*) patterns. In the case that rank(*X*) = *N*<sub><em>x</em></sub>, as is the case when learning mappings from *S* to *P* and vice-versa (Sec. D.1, D.2), the number of learned patterns that can be perfectly reconstucted is simply *N*<sub><em>x</em></sub>.

Thus, learning mappings from sensory cells to either velocity representations or memory palace task items will be exactly successful for up to *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> velocities of memory palace items provided that the mappings are being learned from patterns that form a full rank matrix. However, as seen in [Fig. 6](#F6), mappings must be learned from the reconstructed sensory states rater than the ground truth sensory states, since with increasing number of stored patterns the reconstructed states deviate from the ground truth states.

Thus, even if the sensory states form a full rank matrix, for successful mappings, it will be necessary that the rank of the recovered sensory states must be *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub>. Following the results presented in Sec. D.3, it would appear that the the recovered sensory patterns would form a matrix of rank *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>, the dimensionality of the hyperplane 𝒫<sub><em>H</em></sub>. However, this would only be the case if the recovered sensory states were obtained directly from *W*<sub><em>sh</em></sub>*H* wihtout any additional nonlinearity. For the case of binary sensory states, the recovered sensory patterns are given by sgn\[*W*<sub><em>sh</em></sub>*H*\]. This sign nonlinearity in effect behaves like a small random perturbation to each of the *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> bits of *W*<sub><em>sh</em></sub>*H*, rendering the reconstructed sensory states to be full rank (assuming that the ground-truth sensory states matrix is full rank). Thus, reconstruction of velocity mappings and memory palace task items (or indeed any other readout from sensory the sensory cells) will be successful for up to *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> patterns, provided that the mappings are being learned from the reconstructed sensory states (rather than the ground truth sensory states).

The number of synapses with increasing number of modules were estimated based on a number of hippocampal cells extrapolated from [Fig. 2f](#F2). The number of fixed points increases as a power law with the number of grid cells at a fixed number of modules, with the power law exponent increasing with the number of modules.

We calculate the capacity of the network as the largest number of trained patterns such that all trained patterns are stored as fixed points. Note that this capacity is limited by the number of hippocampal cells, as might be expected from Hopfield like capacity bounds.")

Figure S3. Scaffold constructed with bi-directional learning between grid states and sparse hippocampal states has low capacity

As seen in [Fig. 2f](#F2) *inset*, construction of random sparse hippocampal states with bidirectional learning between grid and hippocampal states results in a scaffold that exibhits catastrophic forgetting. We calculate the capacity of the network as the largest number of trained patterns such that all trained patterns are stored as fixed points. Note that this capacity is limited by the number of hippocampal cells, as might be expected from Hopfield like capacity bounds.

Figure S6. Hebbian learning between sensory layer and scaffold also produces memory continuum

A memory continuum is obtained in Vector-HaSH even if the weights between the sensory and hippocampal layers are bi-directionally trained using Hebbian learning (instead of pseudoinverse learning, as in [Fig. 3](#F3). This continuum is also asymptotically proportional to the theoretical bound on memory capacity (forest green dashed line indicative of slope of theoretical upper bound, vertical and horizontal position of dashed line is arbitrary). However, the proportionality constant is lower, with the gradual degradation of information recall occurring well before *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub>. Vector-HaSH parameters identical to [Fig. 3c](#F3) with *λ* = {3, 4, 5}.

D.1, the number of sensory cells determines the number of scaffold states that can be exactly recovered through the sensory-to-hippocampal weights. For Ns less than the total number of scaffold states, the obtained memory continuum is distorted towards the tail for larger than Ns patterns stored. For all Ns larger than or equal to the number of scaffold states, the memory continuum is identical, corresponding to the results shown in Fig. 3.")

Figure S7. Effect of varying *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> on memory continuum

As shown in SI Sec. D.1, the number of sensory cells determines the number of scaffold states that can be exactly recovered through the sensory-to-hippocampal weights. For *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> less than the total number of scaffold states, the obtained memory continuum is distorted towards the tail for larger than *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> patterns stored. For all *N*<sub><em>s</em></sub> larger than or equal to the number of scaffold states, the memory continuum is identical, corresponding to the results shown in [Fig. 3](#F3).

[![Figure S8.](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Figure S8.](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F16.medium.gif)

](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F16.large.jpg?width=800&height=600&carousel=1 "Basin structure for recovery of sensory hippocampal and grid states varies with number of stored patterns While the scaffold has a large number of well-strucutred basins (cf. Fig. 2, SI Sec. C.1), the basins for sensory recovery are additionally governed by the heteroassociative leanring between the sensory states and the scaffold. As a result, the basin sizes reduce with increasingly large number of stored patterns, due to overcrowding of the number of stored states within the sensory-to-hippocampal weights. The grid periods were set to λ = {3, 4, 5}, with Nh = 400, resulting in a maximal scaffold capacity of 3600 patterns, with perfect sensory recovery up to 400 patterns. For more than 400 stored patterns, p(correct) refers to the probability of exact recovery of grid and hippocampal states, and probability of reliable recovery of the sensory state (which is not exact due to being in the memory continuum regime, Fig. 3.")

- [Download figure](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F16.large.jpg?download=true "Download Figure S8.")
- [Open in new tab](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F16.large.jpg)

Figure S8. Basin structure for recovery of sensory hippocampal and grid states varies with number of stored patterns

While the scaffold has a large number of well-strucutred basins (cf. [Fig. 2](#F2), SI Sec. C.1), the basins for sensory recovery are additionally governed by the heteroassociative leanring between the sensory states and the scaffold. As a result, the basin sizes reduce with increasingly large number of stored patterns, due to overcrowding of the number of stored states within the sensory-to-hippocampal weights. The grid periods were set to *λ* = {3, 4, 5}, with *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> = 400, resulting in a maximal scaffold capacity of 3600 patterns, with perfect sensory recovery up to 400 patterns. For more than 400 stored patterns, *p*(*correct*) refers to the probability of exact recovery of grid and hippocampal states, and probability of reliable recovery of the sensory state (which is not exact due to being in the memory continuum regime, [Fig. 3](#F3).

[![Figure S9.](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Figure S9.](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F17.medium.gif)

](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F17.large.jpg?width=800&height=600&carousel=1 "Vector-HaSH outperforms Autoencoders, particularly when recovering from noisy cues Left When cued with ground-truth memorized sensory patterns, Vector-HaSH recovers a gradually degraded amount of information per pattern (cf. Fig. 3), unlike the memory cliff shown by tailbiting Autoencoders trained as associative memories86. Naively however, it appears that this memory cliff is absent in a single step (i.e., non-tailbiting) of the Autoencoder. However, we see in Right that single-step Autoencoders are not associative memories, since they are unable to reconstruct memories from corrupted cues. Here grid periods were set to λ = {3, 4, 5}, with Nh = 400. Stored sensory cues were random binary { − 1, 1} patterns, and noisy cues were generated by flipping 10% of bits from a given memorized sensory pattern.")

Figure S9. Vector-HaSH outperforms Autoencoders, particularly when recovering from noisy cues

*Left* When cued with ground-truth memorized sensory patterns, Vector-HaSH recovers a gradually degraded amount of information per pattern (cf. [Fig. 3](#F3)), unlike the memory cliff shown by tailbiting Autoencoders trained as associative memories<sup><a id="xref-ref-86-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">86</a></sup>. Naively however, it appears that this memory cliff is absent in a single step (i.e., non-tailbiting) of the Autoencoder. However, we see in *Right* that single-step Autoencoders are not associative memories, since they are unable to reconstruct memories from corrupted cues. Here grid periods were set to *λ* = {3, 4, 5}, with *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> = 400. Stored sensory cues were random binary { − 1, 1} patterns, and noisy cues were generated by flipping 10% of bits from a given memorized sensory pattern.

[![Figure S10.](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Figure S10.](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F18.medium.gif)

](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F18.large.jpg?width=800&height=600&carousel=1 "hippocampal states form a strongly full rank matrix Rank of the Nh × Npatts hippocampal states matrix for varying number of patterns Npatts for two different random permutations of the ordering of hippocampal states shown in black and grey. For up to Npatts ≤ Nh the rank of the matrix is Npatts (as indicated by the red y = x line), and is there after Nh for larger numbers of patterns.")

- [Download figure](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F18.large.jpg?download=true "Download Figure S10.")
- [Open in new tab](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F18.large.jpg)

Figure S10. hippocampal states form a strongly full rank matrix

Rank of the *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> × *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> hippocampal states matrix for varying number of patterns *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> for two different random permutations of the ordering of hippocampal states shown in black and grey. For up to *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> ≤ *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> the rank of the matrix is *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> (as indicated by the red *y* = *x* line), and is there after *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> for larger numbers of patterns.

[![Figure S11.](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Figure S11.](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F19.medium.gif)

](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F19.large.jpg?width=800&height=600&carousel=1 "Activation threshold applied in the hippocampal layer dictates nature of memory continuum (a) distribution of pre-nonlinearity inputs to the hippocampal layer from grid cells. Any actication threshold above the largest value (∼ 7.5) results in zero hippocampal activity, and any threshold below the smallest value (∼− 7.5) results in a purely linear hippocampal layer. (b) A linear hippocampal layer (corresponding to thresholds in the grey region) results in a HPC states matrix of rank equal to the rank of the grid cell states matrix (which equals Ng − M + 1 as shown in Ref.136), whereas a range of thresholds (shown in green) result in a full rank HPC states matrix. (c)Top: The rank of the Nh × Npatts submatrix of hippocampal states constructed over the first Npatts. Here the hippocampal states have been ordered according to the optimal order that leads to fastest scaffold learning generalization (Sec. C.4). Bottom: Rank versus Npatts for the particular values of thresholds considered in panel (d). At a threshold of 0.5 (the value used in almost all simulations in the main text, see Methods for more details) we see that the hpc states matrix is strongly full rank. Moreover, as seen in Fig. S10, this matrix is strongly full rank independent of the ordering of the scaffold states. At the lowest threshold value, corresponding to a linear hippocampal layer, the matrix appears to be rank ordered. However, for a linear hippocampal layer the rank ordering of the matrix is dependent on the ordering of the scaffold states, as examined in Fig. S12. (d) Information recovered per input bit as a function of the number of patterns stored in the network (similar to Fig. 3d) for varying threshold values on a linear scale (top) and a logarithmic scale (bottom). The strongly full rank matrix (identical the the λ = {3, 4, 5} curve in Fig. 3d) and the rank ordered matrix both demonstrate perfect recovery up to a knee; all values of thresholds result in a smooth decay of recovered information that is asymptotically proportional to a theoretically expected bound that scales inversely with the number of stored patterns")

- [Download figure](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F19.large.jpg?download=true "Download Figure S11.")
- [Open in new tab](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F19.large.jpg)

Figure S11. Activation threshold applied in the hippocampal layer dictates nature of memory continuum

(a) distribution of pre-nonlinearity inputs to the hippocampal layer from grid cells. Any actication threshold above the largest value (∼ 7.5) results in zero hippocampal activity, and any threshold below the smallest value (∼− 7.5) results in a purely linear hippocampal layer. (b) A linear hippocampal layer (corresponding to thresholds in the grey region) results in a HPC states matrix of rank equal to the rank of the grid cell states matrix (which equals *N*<sub><em>g</em></sub> − *M* + 1 as shown in Ref.<sup><a id="xref-ref-136-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-136">136</a></sup>), whereas a range of thresholds (shown in green) result in a full rank HPC states matrix. (c)*Top*: The rank of the *N*<sub><em>h</em></sub> × *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub> submatrix of hippocampal states constructed over the first *N*<sub><em>patts</em></sub>. Here the hippocampal states have been ordered according to the optimal order that leads to fastest scaffold learning generalization (Sec. C.4). *Bottom*: Rank versus *N*<sub><em>p</em></sub>*atts* for the particular values of thresholds considered in panel (d). At a threshold of 0.5 (the value used in almost all simulations in the main text, see *Methods* for more details) we see that the hpc states matrix is strongly full rank. Moreover, as seen in [Fig. S10](#F18), this matrix is strongly full rank independent of the ordering of the scaffold states. At the lowest threshold value, corresponding to a linear hippocampal layer, the matrix appears to be rank ordered. However, for a linear hippocampal layer the rank ordering of the matrix is dependent on the ordering of the scaffold states, as examined in [Fig. S12](#F20). (d) Information recovered per input bit as a function of the number of patterns stored in the network (similar to [Fig. 3d](#F3)) for varying threshold values on a linear scale (*top*) and a logarithmic scale (*bottom*). The strongly full rank matrix (identical the the *λ* = {3, 4, 5} curve in [Fig. 3d](#F3)) and the rank ordered matrix both demonstrate perfect recovery up to a knee; all values of thresholds result in a smooth decay of recovered information that is asymptotically proportional to a theoretically expected bound that scales inversely with the number of stored patterns

[![Figure S12.](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Figure S12.](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F20.medium.gif)

](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F20.large.jpg?width=800&height=600&carousel=1 "Linear hippocampal layer generates memory continuum only for specific ordering of scaffold states during learning (a) Three examples of potential ordering of scaffold states that could be considered: top left the discontinuous path that leads to fastest scaffold learning generalization (Sec. C.4) shown for λ = {3, 4, 5} ; top right a continuous spiral path; bottom a continuous ‘hairpin’ path. (b) The hpc states submatrix is rank ordered along the optimal path, and approximately rank ordered for the continuous path. The hairpin path however is significantly deviated from a rank ordered matrix. (c) Information recovered per input bit as a function of the number of patterns stored in the network (similar to Fig. 3d). The rank ordered matrix demonstrates perfect recoverey up to a knee at the rank of the grid states matrix; this is also closely approximated by the spiral ordered matrix. A hairpin ordering however results in poor information recovery even at a small number of patterns. In all cases, the asymptotic decay of information is inversely proportional to the number of patterns, as would be expected from theoretical information bounds.")

- [Download figure](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F20.large.jpg?download=true "Download Figure S12.")
- [Open in new tab](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F20.large.jpg)

Figure S12. Linear hippocampal layer generates memory continuum only for specific ordering of scaffold states during learning

(a) Three examples of potential ordering of scaffold states that could be considered: *top left* the discontinuous path that leads to fastest scaffold learning generalization (Sec. C.4) shown for *λ* = {3, 4, 5} ; *top right* a continuous spiral path; *bottom* a continuous ‘hairpin’ path. (b) The hpc states submatrix is rank ordered along the optimal path, and approximately rank ordered for the continuous path. The hairpin path however is significantly deviated from a rank ordered matrix. (c) Information recovered per input bit as a function of the number of patterns stored in the network (similar to [Fig. 3d](#F3)). The rank ordered matrix demonstrates perfect recoverey up to a knee at the rank of the grid states matrix; this is also closely approximated by the spiral ordered matrix. A hairpin ordering however results in poor information recovery even at a small number of patterns. In all cases, the asymptotic decay of information is inversely proportional to the number of patterns, as would be expected from theoretical information bounds.

[![Figure S13.](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Figure S13.](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F21.medium.gif)

](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F21.large.jpg?width=800&height=600&carousel=1 "Mean activity in hippocampal layer can be used for novelty detection The mean activity in the hippocampal layer for familiar patterns presents a narrow distribution. The mean hippocampal activity for novel patterns is strongly dependent on the number of stored patterns. The narrowness of the familar pattern distribution allows for discrimination thresholds to be placed on either side (at two standard deviations away from the mean) to result in classification accuracy as shown in Fig. 3i")

- [Download figure](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F21.large.jpg?download=true "Download Figure S13.")
- [Open in new tab](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F21.large.jpg)

Figure S13. Mean activity in hippocampal layer can be used for novelty detection

The mean activity in the hippocampal layer for familiar patterns presents a narrow distribution. The mean hippocampal activity for novel patterns is strongly dependent on the number of stored patterns. The narrowness of the familar pattern distribution allows for discrimination thresholds to be placed on either side (at two standard deviations away from the mean) to result in classification accuracy as shown in [Fig. 3i](#F3)

[![Figure S14.](data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7)

![Figure S14.](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F22.medium.gif)

](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F22.large.jpg?width=800&height=600&carousel=1 "Sensory driven transitions must be reconstructed from recalled states (a) Architecture for sensory based reconstruction of next-step transitions for sequence learning, Fig. 6a, bottom. (b) As the number of stored patterns increases, the recalled sensory state gradually degrades; as a result, reconstruction from mapping trained on ground truth sensory states can lead to inaccuracies. (c) More quantitatively, transitions trained on recalled sensory states result in sequence reconstruction of length up to the number of sensory cells (theory in SI Sec. D.6), whereas transitions trained on ground truth sensory states has a lower sequence capacity.")

- [Download figure](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F22.large.jpg?download=true "Download Figure S14.")
- [Open in new tab](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/biorxiv/early/2023/11/28/2023.11.28.568960/F22.large.jpg)

Figure S14. Sensory driven transitions must be reconstructed from recalled states

(a) Architecture for sensory based reconstruction of next-step transitions for sequence learning, [Fig. 6a](#F6), *bottom*. (b) As the number of stored patterns increases, the recalled sensory state gradually degrades; as a result, reconstruction from mapping trained on ground truth sensory states can lead to inaccuracies. (c) More quantitatively, transitions trained on recalled sensory states result in sequence reconstruction of length up to the number of sensory cells (theory in SI Sec. D.6), whereas transitions trained on ground truth sensory states has a lower sequence capacity.

Left: Results when only Wsh weights are reinforced, assuming pre-trained scaffold weights Wgh. Right: Results when all of the learnable weights in Vector-HaSH Whs, Wsh and Wgh are reinforced. Note that Whs reinforcement mathematically doesn’t change Whs as describe in Sec. 1.")

Figure S15. Reconstruction error in each layer of Vector-HaSH when tested for MTT by reinforcing the model weights for a subset of repeated patterns.

## Footnotes

- [↵](#xref-fn-2-1)\* fiete{at}mit.edu
- <sup>1</sup> We show in SI Sec. D.5 that one could simply use Hebbian learning instead of an iterative or standard pseudoinverse learning, while maintaining the same asymptotic capacity, with a smaller constant pre-factor, [Fig. S6](#F14)
- <sup>2</sup> Since input sensory patterns are stored via association to scaffold states, once all scaffold states have been associated with an input there are three possibilities. The first is that the memory is saturated and no further sensory patterns can be stored. The second involves selecting an existing scaffold state randomly or based on sensory overlap and rewriting the previous memory’s heteroassociative weights to accommodate the present sensory input. The third involves a gradual decay of the heteroassociative weights between sensory inputs and the scaffold, so that older memories are lost and those scaffold states are identified for reuse.
- <sup>3</sup> Had we not earlier assumed that elements of *W* are drawn from a normal distribution we would have arrived at this same result with different intermediate distribution instead of *χ*<sup>2</sup> and 𝒩 𝒫

Comments

bioRxiv aims to provide a venue for anyone to comment on a bioRxiv preprint. Comments are moderated for offensive or irrelevant content (this can take ~24 h). Please avoid duplicate submissions. The content of a comment is not endorsed by bioRxiv. bioRxiv is now using a new commenting interface powered by Hypothes.is. All comments previously submitted via the legacy platform (Disqus) have been transferred to the new platform using the bioRxiv\_admin account. Please read our [commenting guide](https://connect.biorxiv.org/news/2022/03/21/commenting_on_preprints) for more information.

TRiP

bioRxiv partners with journals and review services to enable posting of peer reviews and editorial decisions related to preprints they are evaluating. Reviews are posted with the consent of the authors.

TRiP reviews are part of a peer review pilot project in which participating organizations post peer reviews of manuscripts they are evaluating

*There is no TRiP material for this paper.*

Automated Services

A variety of services now perform automated analyses of papers. Outputs from automated tools that summarize and extract information from bioRxiv preprints using AI and other technologies are displayed below. Note these tools can generate errors and the information has not been verified by bioRxiv or the authors.

Automated services are part of a pilot project .....

### AI-generated summary from [ScienceCast](https://sciencecast.org/)

Select level:

General

Expert

  

A new memory model called Vector-HaSH has been developed, which has the ability to store and recall patterns with high accuracy. The model utilizes a grid-hippocampal scaffold and can map sensory inputs to corresponding grid and hippocampal states. It exhibits high-capacity pattern reconstruction, even in the presence of noise. Vector-HaSH can accurately recall stored sensory inputs in different spatial environments and can learn and recall spatial trajectories. The model also supports sequence memory and exhibits high-capacity general associative memory, spatial memory, and episodic memory. This new model provides insights into the mechanisms of memory and has potential applications in various fields.

[Explore further on ScienceCast](https://sciencecast.org/casts/gto1fzsp35na)
